==PAGE:4==
Abstract
Marketing has experienced a fundamental shift in recent years. The methods and ideas
that were effective for half a century have become outdated, except for the essential prin-
ciple that the customer’s opinion is paramount. The key to achieving business success
today is directly related to how closely we listen to the customer. Companies can do so
by utilising data to a greater extent, and thus, data-driven marketing is becoming the new
norm. However, data-driven marketing demands acquiring and mastering new technol-
ogy and therefore creates a barrier for smaller companies with smaller budgets and fewer
resources. With this thesis, we seek to understand how startup companies can become
more data-driven in their marketing approach. To understand this, a case study approach
was employed and we interviewed ten marketing leaders in Danish startup companies.
As a result, we have identified both barriers and enabling factors to become more data-
driven. We have employed the Gioia methodology for constructing a grounded model,
which highlights the most relevant findings of the interview data. By incorporating relevant
marketing, technology and entrepreneurial literature, we finally present recommendations
for how startup companies can execute a data-driven marketing strategy that is tied to the
organisational goals.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
iii

Glossary
Word
Definition
Omnichannel
marketing
A consistent and integrated customer experience strategy
across multiple offline and online customer touchpoints, util-
ising integrated data points.
Segmentation
Segmentation is the division of a market into smaller parts – or
segments.
First-party data
Refers to customer information that a company collects directly
from its own interactions and transactions with customers,
which can be used for marketing purposes.
Second-party data
Customer information that is collected as first-party data by a
trusted partner, rather than collected directly from own cus-
tomer interactions and transactions.
Third-party data
Customer information that is acquired from external sources,
such as large data aggregators, and used for marketing pur-
poses.
Big data
Exceptionally large and complex data sets that require ad-
vanced processing technology to computationally extract
meaningful insights and patterns.
Growth team
A distinct, data-driven team of employees dedicated to in-
crease the rate of customer acquisition, retention, and revenue
by optimising products and inventing new marketing channels.
The team operate in the intersection of marketing, product de-
velopment and sales.
Data point
A data point is a single unit of data within a larger dataset that
can be analysed or quantified.
North Star metric
A single metric that a business uses to measure its success
and guide decision-making towards a common goal.
Source of truth
A single, authoritative data source that is trusted to be accurate
and reliable for making decisions.
Marketing
technology
The definition of marketing technology in this thesis is defined
as tools and software that can relate both to the product and
the service that follows.
Vanity metric
A gross quantity metric that appears impressive as a KPI, but
lacks context, have unclear intent and that do not guide action
and learning.

1.1
Introduction and background
The internet has greatly impacted the global business landscape on the brink of the 21st
century with the rise of digital transformation. [1] With the digital transformation, digital
technology has gotten incorporated into all areas of business, fundamentally altering how
businesses operate and deliver value to customers. This has further prompted a cultural
change that has required organisations to continually experiment and challenge the status
quo to stay competitive. This metamorphosis has led to an evolution of traditional market-
ing; from a field closely related to communications to a broader discipline encompassing
scientific, technological, and strategic domains. [2]
Thus, digital transformation has played a critical role in the development of data-driven
marketing. Data-driven marketing can best be described as a discipline that utilises data to
inform and optimise marketing initiatives and decision-making by undertaking a customer-
centric methodology. This is accomplished by providing the tools and technologies nec-
essary to collect, process, and analyse large amounts of data, as well as to automate and
personalise marketing efforts based on this data. In the digital age, this customer-centric
approach to marketing is becoming crucial for businesses of all sizes and sectors.
However, becoming data-driven in marketing does not come without its challenges, espe-
cially for startup companies that have limited experience with data analytics or are facing
constraints on their time and resources. However, it is particularly important for startups
to overcome these challenges, as they will have to optimise their marketing efforts to
establish themselves in new markets and differentiate themselves from competitors.
In this thesis, we aim to investigate the main challenges that startup companies face in
their pursuit to elevate their marketing efforts and results by becoming more data-driven
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
1
==PAGE:11==
in their marketing approach. To do this, we conducted interviews with 10 Danish startup
companies to gather insights and perspectives on their experiences with data-driven mar-
keting.
We do not consider it possible to account for the entire marketing machine of the company
and conduct a complete analysis of where, how, and when data affects all the different
sub-elements. Instead, we focus on the more general elements of business marketing
and highlight the aspects that are most prominent in connection with companies’ use of
data-driven marketing.
In addition to exploring the challenges faced by startups, we create an overview of the
most relevant recent marketing technologies that startups can utilise to improve their data-
driven marketing efforts. In order to identify the most promising and innovative state-of-
the-art technologies in the field of data-driven marketing, a systematic literature review
has been conducted.
Using collected data from both interviews and literature review, we identify a set of best
practices and form recommendations on how startups can solve challenges to become
more data-driven in their marketing efforts. This will include not only advice on the tech-
nologies and strategies that may be most effective for startups but also considerations for
overcoming common challenges and barriers to success.
Finally, we will investigate the ethical consequences of using data-driven marketing tech-
nologies and strategies. This is accomplished through a review of extant literature on
digital and data-driven marketing as well as expert interviews. This allows us to con-
sider the potential risks and responsibilities of using customer data to inform marketing
initiatives.
Overall, this thesis aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the challenges
and opportunities that Danish startups face as they seek to elevate their marketing re-
sults through a data-driven approach. By identifying best practices and providing recom-
mendations for success, we hope to contribute to the body of knowledge on data-driven
marketing and assist startups in their pursuit of growth and success.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
2
==PAGE:12==
1.2
Research question
Extensive research has been conducted on digital marketing strategies developed by
SMEs as well as how SMEs should improve their digital marketing strategies. In 2021,
the Journal of Small Business Management furthermore published a research paper on
Digital marketing in SMEs via data-driven strategies. [3]
However, much less research has been done investigating startup companies. In fact,
there are currently no published research papers concerning data-driven marketing in
startups.
Startup companies are interesting to investigate because they are separated from SMEs
by the fact that they are formed to launch new products in the market. [4] Furthermore,
data-driven marketing is on the rise and with the continuous development of emerging
technologies, we believe that this research could be of significant relevance to the startup
community.
While SMEs are defined based on their number of employees, annual turnover and/or
balance sheet total [5], there is no single universally accepted definition of a startup [6].
This is perhaps the reason for the lack of well-defined research on the topic. Thus, for the
purpose of writing this thesis, we are specifying our own definition in section 3.3.2. This
is the definition we refer to throughout the entire thesis.
1.2.1
Research question
In this thesis, we wish to investigate the main challenges that Danish startups are facing
in their pursuit to elevate their marketing data maturity levels and become data-driven in
their marketing approach.
Secondly, we wish to create an overview of the most relevant recent technologies that
can be utilised by startups for this purpose.
We wish to make use of the collected data to identify best practices and form recommen-
dations on how startups can approach becoming data-driven in their marketing efforts.
The tentative research question is:
How can Danish startups elevate their marketing efforts and
results by undertaking a data-driven marketing approach?
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
3
==PAGE:13==
The research question contains multiple relevant aspects which we will need to uncover
before we can determine how a data-driven marketing approach can elevate the market-
ing efforts of Danish startup companies. The research question can therefore further be
divided into a number of sub-questions:
1. What are the main barriers and enabling factors that startups are encountering when
working with data-driven marketing?
2. How can the identified barriers be addressed and resolved?
3. What recent digital trends and technologies can further enhance data-driven mar-
keting in startups?
4.
When we have gained the above insights, we will be able to provide practical recom-
mendations on how companies can achieve a more data-driven marketing approach, so
that ICPs are more likely to engage in companies’ communications, become customers,
and develop loyalty. Our recommendations constitute the result of the study and analy-
sis, which companies can use as guidelines and inspiration for performing and optimising
their data-driven marketing efforts.
1.2.2
Motivation
Our motivation for writing this thesis derives from our interest in exploring the trends and
possibilities that technological advancements bring. Specifically in relation to market-
ing, where technology and data constantly change the conditions and opportunities for
communication between businesses and consumers. We are particularly interested in
understanding how startups can leverage data-driven marketing to achieve better results
and compete more effectively in their respective markets. The chosen focus on startup
companies originates from the notion that these are more challenged in adopting new
technology due to their constraints on budget and resources, but also the uncertainty
about the viability of their proposed business model. [4] Upon initiating the research for
this thesis, we were also curious to determine if there are factors that could prove to be an
advantage for the startups and if these factors, in that case, can be employed to an even
greater extent. One hypothesis we had prior to the process, was that the trial-and-error
approach which startups often undertake, would prove beneficial when working with data-
driven marketing strategies, as these are based on the same principle. As a large part
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
4
==PAGE:14==
of new startup companies similarly are technology-based, we expected that this would
be another advantage for the startups, and were curious to explore what enabling factors
could be identified.

The purpose of this chapter is to provide the reader with a clear understanding of the
research context, including the history and evolution of data-driven marketing, as well as
relevant theories and concepts. The theory presented in this chapter will, together with
the collected interview data, serve as the foundation for the analysis, which will ultimately
aid in answering the tentative research questions.
2.1
Traditional marketing
To understand the distinction between data-driven marketing and traditional marketing
techniques, it is necessary to undertake a retrospective analysis. This entails revisiting
the origins of marketing as a discipline.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
6
==PAGE:16==
Table 2.1: Traditional Marketing compared to Data-driven Marketing
Traditional Marketing

Takes place offline
Primarily takes place online

From the middle of the 20th century, marketing evolved from a sales-orientation focus
into a formal discipline with the establishment of marketing associations and academic
programs. Businesses began to adopt a customer-centric approach to marketing strate-
gies by utilising market research to better target customer needs and specific consumer
groups. The post-World War II era was characterised by increased competition for con-
sumers’ discretionary income, making marketing even more crucial. Companies adopted
segmentation and positioning strategies to distinguish their products and services from
their competitors.
At its core, marketing has always been focused on achieving two crucial objectives. The
first objective is to determine the wants and needs of customers. The second objective
is to leverage this knowledge to create and deliver goods or services that will be pur-
chased by those customers. With traditional marketing, brands have been looking for a
way to spread awareness of their products and brand. Employing traditional marketing,
this was done with one-way communication through print advertising and direct mail. Sub-
sequently, through radio, TV ads, and video commercials. With the advancement of the
internet, email marketing eventually replaced direct mail, whereas the other channels are
still utilised by marketers today. [7][8]
Traditional marketing relies on three methods to define the wants and needs of customers:
Market studies, historical ”data” from past sales and assumptions about the target audi-
ence.
In the 21st century, globalisation and digital technology dramatically transformed the way
companies approach marketing. The discipline of Digital Marketing emerged along with
techniques such as social media marketing, search engine optimisation, and search en-
gine advertising. However, with big data and emerging technology becoming more preva-
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
7
==PAGE:17==
lent across industries, marketing continues to develop to be more targeted and specific
across many different potential channels.
2.2
Data-driven marketing
Data-driven marketing is a modern approach to marketing that relies on data science
to inform decision-making and guide marketing efforts. [9] This approach involves col-
lecting, analysing, and utilising vast amounts of customer data, such as demographics,
purchase history, and online behaviour, to create highly targeted and personalised mar-
keting strategies and campaigns. In a data-driven marketing strategy, customer data is
analysed using advanced analytics tools and algorithms to uncover insights and patterns.
This information is then used to develop targeted campaigns and customer journeys that
are tailored to meet the needs and preferences of specific customer segments. [10]
The goal of data-driven marketing is to provide customers with a more personalised and
engaging experience, leading to increased customer loyalty and customer lifetime value.
By leveraging data and analytics, data-driven marketing allows marketers to make more
informed decisions, optimise their marketing spend and improve the return on investment
(ROI) of their marketing efforts. When done right, data-driven marketing can lead to re-
fined customer experiences and inform product development. Furthermore, it can enable
customer-oriented techniques such as personalisation and omnichannel marketing, which
we will elaborate further on later in this chapter.
Data-driven marketing represents a significant shift in the way that marketers approach
customer engagement, offering a data-driven, customer-centric approach that delivers
measurable results and improved marketing efficiency.
2.2.1
Data science
Data science plays a crucial role in data-driven marketing by providing the tools and tech-
niques necessary to analyse and make sense of the vast amounts of customer data that
are collected. Methods such as machine learning, predictive modelling, and statistical
analysis are utilised to extract insights and patterns from customer data. This information
is then used to inform marketing strategy and decision-making, such as identifying cus-
tomer segments, predicting customer behaviour, and optimising marketing campaigns,
as aforementioned.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
8
==PAGE:18==
Data science can be defined as ”(...) an interdisciplinary field that employs methods, pro-
cesses, and algorithms to collect, identify, and extract insights from structured or unstruc-
tured databases. Data science is characterized by multiple and varied uses of artificial
intelligence.” [3].
As an example, machine learning algorithms can be used to predict customer churn,
enabling marketers to take proactive measures to retain customers before they leave.
Predictive modelling may also be used to forecast the success of marketing campaigns,
allowing marketers to optimize their spending and improve campaign performance. Data
science also plays a role in the personalisation of marketing efforts by using customer
data to create targeted and relevant experiences for each individual customer. For exam-
ple, data scientists may use customer purchase history, browsing behaviour, and demo-
graphic information to recommend personalised products and offers to each customer.
2.3
Relevant marketing concepts
2.3.1
Customer Journey
The Customer Journey refers to the journey a customer takes from initial brand awareness
to engagement and the resulting perception of the interaction. This encompasses the
complete range of experiences and actions that a customer undergoes while engaging
with a company and its brand. The process of mapping out the customer journey plays a
crucial role in defining, developing, and enhancing a company’s customer experience with
the brand. [11] Understanding the customer journey is therefore a valuable and critical
aspect, however also complicated. As each customer follows a unique path, journey data
is diverse. Nevertheless, sophisticated models possess the capability to not only identify
the structure, trends, and patterns embedded in the customer journey but also convert
them into actionable insights. [2] The outcome of a customer journey is what is known as
the customer experience. It is the accumulated impression that the customer has after
having interacted with the brand through different touchpoints and different stages of a
Customer Journey.
In figure 2.1 technological research and consulting firm, Gartner, illustrates the mapping of
a buying journey for a B2B supplier. Gartner has identified six tasks which the customer
must complete to satisfaction for the customer to purchase the product. These steps
are Problem identification, Solution exploration, Requirements building/overview, Supplier
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
9
==PAGE:19==
Figure 2.1: Gartner: Example of mapping of B2B Customer Journey until purchase stage
selection, Validation and Consensus creation. Throughout the first four steps, the supplier
in question can affect the customer through different brand touchpoints. These four steps
are illustrated in the figure, in the orange squares. The two last steps, however, are not
illustrated in the figure, as these steps are taken without any interference from the supplier
in question. What Gartner want to illustrate with this, is that the customer can include
multiple stakeholders. This is often the case for B2B suppliers, although not exclusively.
With multiple stakeholders, the customer journey becomes even more complex. Another
thing to be aware of is that the customer journey is not a linear process. Rather, customers
engage in a looping behaviour across the six buying tasks, revisiting each task multiple
times. [12]
According to Gartner’s survey of 750 respondents, customers who perceived the infor-
mation provided by suppliers as useful in facilitating progress across their buying tasks
had a three-fold increase in the likelihood of purchasing a larger deal with less regret.
[12] This emphasises the value companies can gain from mapping out, understanding
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
10
==PAGE:20==
and enhancing their customer journeys. This also stresses the significance of prioritising
marketing efforts in a manner that reaches the customers on all levels of the marketing
funnel.
2.3.2
Ideal Customer Profile
An Ideal Customer Profile (ICP) is a detailed description of the perfect customer for a
company. It is a tool employed by businesses to identify and target their most valuable
customers. The ICP is created based on a deep understanding of the company’s products
or services, the market, and the needs of the target audience. [13]
An ICP typically includes a detailed description of the customer’s demographic, psycho-
graphic, and behavioural attributes. Demographic information includes factors such as
age, gender, income, education level, and location. To utilise demographic information
for the ICP, companies can access first-party data from their existing customers through
web analytics, surveys, sign-up forms, or loyalty programs. Second-party data can be ac-
cessed through social media platforms, such as Facebook and LinkedIn, and third-party
data through third-party data providers. Companies can purchase demographic data from
third-party data providers such as Experian, or Acxiom, which collect and aggregate data
from various sources such as surveys, census data, and public records.
Psychographic information includes factors such as personality traits, values, beliefs, and
attitudes. Similarly to demographic data, psychographic information can be accessed
through social media platforms. Social media platforms provide insights into users’ inter-
ests, hobbies, and preferences, which can be used to infer their psychographic attributes.
Companies can also collect psychographic data from their existing customers through
surveys, website forms, or customer support interactions.
Behavioural information includes factors such as purchase history, buying habits, and
preferred communication channels. For eCommerce companies, this can be accessed
through web analytics. Companies can use website analytics tools such as Google Ana-
lytics to track user behaviour on their website, including pages visited, time spent, and ac-
tions taken. For behavioural data, companies can also conduct surveys and focus groups
to gather data on their target audience’s attitudes, beliefs, values, and motivations.
Once the common characteristics have been identified, they can be used to create a
detailed profile of the ideal customer. ICPs are a strategic guide and a crucial tool for
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
11
==PAGE:21==
marketers to develop and execute a data-driven marketing strategy.[13]
2.3.3
Buyer Persona and segmentation
A customer persona is also known as a ”Buyer persona” and refers to a semi-fictional,
imaginative representation of the ideal customer of a company. It is a tool used by com-
panies to understand their target audience better and create more effective marketing
strategies. However, they are not only used by marketers. Organisations can utilise buyer
personas beyond marketing and sales, extending their application to other areas such as
customer service. By providing a fictional representation of the ideal customer, compa-
nies can ensure that all employees throughout the company interact with customers in
the most effective way possible.
Buyer personas can be created through research, interviews, and data analysis to identify
common characteristics and behaviours of a particular group of customers.
Marketers can benefit from using buyer personas in several ways. Firstly, it helps them
to understand their customers’ needs, motivations, and pain points. With this informa-
tion, marketers can tailor their messaging, content, and offerings to better resonate with
their target audience. Additionally, by understanding the different personas within their
target audience, marketers can segment their customer base and create more targeted
marketing campaigns. This can lead to higher engagement, increased conversion rates,
and ultimately, higher revenue.
Buyer persona is closely related to other marketing concepts such as segmentation. Seg-
mentation involves dividing a customer base into groups of individuals who share similar
characteristics or behaviours. Segmentation is essentially the division of a market into
smaller parts – or segments. By identifying these segments, marketers can create tar-
geted marketing campaigns that are more likely to resonate with each group.
Buyer personas are a more detailed version of segmentation, focusing on creating a spe-
cific representation of an individual within a particular segment. Segmentation is often
used as a starting point to develop buyer personas, as it helps to identify the common
characteristics of a particular group of customers. [13]
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
12
==PAGE:22==
Figure 2.2: The AIDA Model: ”Awareness”, ”Interest”, ”Desire” and ”Action”.
2.3.4
The Marketing Funnel
The Marketing and Sales Funnel, also known as the AIDA model, was developed by Elias
St. Elmo Lewis in 1898 to describe how personal selling works. Marketing and sales
professionals have revisited and updated the AIDA model many times since to better
align it with evolving requirements. For instance, the DAGMAR model (which is short for
”defining advertising goals for measured advertising results”) was developed for tracking
progress towards meeting marketing goals. [14]
Although variations from the original AIDA model may prove beneficial for specific niches,
the use of these models can have widespread applications. The primary objective is to
customise messaging to potential customers in targeted ways that encourage them to
proceed through the decision-making process and make a purchase. [14]
The original AIDA model consists of four stages: ”Awareness”, ”Interest”, ”Desire” and
”Action” (figure 2.2). By creating awareness, the customer is introduced to the product or
service and thereby enters the top of the funnel. The awareness is formed by casting a
wide net and creating initial interest. For instance, this could be achieved through branding
activities.
While the marketing funnel has been employed widely for decades in traditional marketing,
it is still used today, also for employing omnichannel and data-driven marketing strategies.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
13
==PAGE:23==
2.4
Technology and digital trends
Consistently, research indicates that companies that heavily invest in new technology
are more agile and have a notable competitive advantage when compared to those that
do not. [15] Thus, utilising new technology for marketing purposes can potentially be
a beneficial way for startups to quickly reach and acquire customers in a new market.
Established companies with a large existing customer base often have time-consuming
standard operating procedures and an IT department with technical requirements. This
hinders rapid implementation and testing of new technologies and software, providing
agile startups with a competitive advantage.
Employing marketing technology produces new data and analytics methods, which can
be used for improving business-to-customer interactions. In figure 2.3, developed by Hoff-
man et al., the continuous feedback loop of data output is illustrated. While the research
by Hoffman et al. focuses entirely on “new” technologies that are not widely adopted in
marketing – such as facial recognition and augmented reality (AR), this is also relevant
for the marketing technologies that have been more broadly employed for the past ten
years. In this thesis, we concentrate on both immature and more mature and widespread
technologies, however, as the barrier of entry for marketing-immature technologies can
be high, there are some technologies which we will not mention.
Figure 2.3: Hoffman et al: New technology improves marketing decision-making
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
14
==PAGE:24==
2.4.1
Marketing technology stack
Marketers today rely on a wide set of marketing technology. There are a variety of com-
monly known tools and software that are being utilised today as a part of what is commonly
known as the “marketing technology stack” or ”MarTech stack”. The marketing technology
stack is a grouping of technologies that supports all marketing functions throughout the
customer journey, providing the following capabilities and benefits:
• Process automation: Marketing automation software can automate otherwise man-
ual processes to incorporate the best marketing practices across all aspects of the
company. Businesses can refine, automate, and operationalise new learning as it
arrives.
• Marketing efficiency: The automation software and technology can be leveraged
to perform historically manual processes faster, more efficiently, and at a lesser
expense. Marketing professionals can spend less time on repetitive tasks and more
time on testing, innovating, and optimising, resulting in greater business agility and
more return on marketing investment.
• Customer data & insights: Modern companies compete on data, which they turn into
insights to gain competitive superiority. Managing marketing processes within the
marketing stack generates data that can be harnessed and enriched with internally
or externally acquired data to better understand customers and improve marketing
operations. [16]
The components of the marketing technology stack differ depending on the company.
For example, a B2C e-commerce company with a large volume of customers will benefit
greatly from marketing automation software with an automatic email flow in place when-
ever a customer exits after adding a product to the basket on their webshop. However,
a B2B company specialising in complex software might rely on a sales team rather than
a webshop. Experienced sales employees possess the expertise to read significant buy-
ing signals in the body language of potential customers. Similarly, data-driven marketing
identifies customers’ specific interests based on their digital behaviour. Through targeted
activities, it can recognise customers and qualify them for direct contact with the sales
team. [17]
Thus, there is no universal solution when it comes to determining the tools of the mar-
keting technology stack. Creating effective marketing technology demands a thorough
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
15
==PAGE:25==
comprehension of the evolving customer behaviour, the channels experiencing growth,
and the reason behind it. Companies must establish their strategies and objectives upon
precise insights into these factors and subsequently create organisational frameworks
and competencies to support them. Only at that point is it possible to determine which
channels and technology to employ. [18]
Conventional marketing technologies include but are not limited to, customer relationship
management (CRM) systems, email marketing tools, social media management tools,
content management systems (CMS), search engine optimization (SEO) tools, web an-
alytics software, and advertising platforms such as Google Ads, LinkedIn Ads and Meta
Ads.
In addition to these standard marketing technologies, data-driven marketing employs
some lesser-known techniques. For instance, predictive analytics uses machine learn-
ing algorithms and statistical models to analyse data and identify patterns that can help
predict future behaviour and trends. Chatbots and virtual assistants are AI-powered tools
that provide real-time personalised recommendations, customer service, and product in-
formation. Interactive content such as quizzes, polls, and surveys encourage customer
interaction and provide valuable insights and data for future marketing efforts. Geofenc-
ing uses geospatial data to create virtual boundaries around physical locations, allowing
marketers to deliver personalised messages or offers to customers when they enter a
specific location. Beacon technology utilises Bluetooth-enabled devices to transmit sig-
nals to nearby smartphones, delivering targeted content or promotions in real time. Lastly,
augmented reality allows marketers to create interactive, 3D experiences that enhance
customer engagement and provide a more immersive brand experience. Additionally,
marketing automation technology automates repetitive marketing tasks such as email
campaigns, social media management, and lead nurturing, freeing up marketers’ time
for strategic and creative work.
While technologies such as geofencing, AI and machine learning have existed for many
years, it is only in recent years that the technologies have been commonly used by mar-
keters. Large language models, such as GPT-3 [19], have only been available since 2020
and are still relatively new in the field of marketing. However, they are already being used
by some companies to generate content, improve customer experience, and personalise
marketing messages. As the technology continues to evolve and become more widely
available, it is likely that its use in marketing will become more common.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
16
==PAGE:26==
In the subsequent subsections, we will expand upon some of the most relevant marketing
technologies.
2.4.2
Marketing automation software
Marketing automation refers to the use of technology to streamline, automate, and mea-
sure marketing tasks and workflows. The primary goal of marketing automation is to
increase efficiency and effectiveness in marketing efforts, freeing up marketers to focus
on other tasks. In a data-driven marketing approach, customer data is collected and anal-
ysed to inform marketing strategy and decision-making. Marketing automation technology
enables organisations to effectively leverage this data to deliver personalised and relevant
experiences to customers at scale.
Marketing automation technology includes a range of tools, such as email marketing, lead
nurturing, lead scoring, and customer segmentation, that allow marketers to automate
routine tasks and processes. Email marketing automation is a commonly used discipline,
which enables marketers to send targeted and personalised emails to customers based
on specific triggers, such as a sign-up or purchase, without having to manually send each
email.
Lead nurturing and lead scoring are also important components of marketing automation,
as they allow marketers to identify and prioritise the most promising leads and target
them with relevant and personalised content. Customer segmentation is another key
component, enabling marketers to segment their audience based on specific attributes,
such as demographics, behaviour, or psychographics, and deliver tailored and relevant
messages to each segment.
Marketing automation technology also provides valuable data and insights, allowing mar-
keters to track and measure the success of their campaigns and make data-driven de-
cisions. For example, by tracking open rates, click-through rates, and conversions to
identify areas for improvement and optimise future campaigns for better results.
2.4.3
Revenue attribution software
Revenue attribution is about understanding which marketing channels and tactics are driv-
ing the most conversions and revenue. For this intent, marketing attribution software is
employed by businesses to gain a more complex understanding of attribution. It functions
by tracking the customer journey across multiple touchpoints and attributing credit to each
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
17
==PAGE:27==
interaction that contributes to a conversion. The software uses various methods to deter-
mine the impact of each marketing touchpoint. This includes first-touch attribution (giving
credit to the first interaction a customer had with the brand), last-touch attribution (giving
credit to the last interaction before conversion), and multi-touch attribution (dividing credit
across multiple touchpoints). By analysing this data, marketing teams can make data-
driven decisions about where to allocate their resources and optimise their campaigns for
maximum impact. Marketing attribution software can also help businesses understand
which channels and tactics are not delivering results, allowing them to make informed
decisions about where to cut spending. Overall, marketing attribution software can be a
powerful tool for businesses looking to improve the effectiveness of their marketing efforts
and drive revenue growth. (Source: https://www.measured.com/faq/what-is-marketing-
attribution-software/)
2.4.4
Data Management Platform
A Data Management Platform (DMP) is a software system that serves as a centralised hub
for managing and organising large volumes of data collected from various sources. The
primary function of DMPs is to gather and analyse data from multiple sources, including
websites, social media platforms, mobile applications, and offline sources. Through this
process, DMPs can create a unified and comprehensive perspective of individual users
or groups of users, which can be utilised to target them with customised advertising mes-
sages. By offering insights into the audience, DMPs enable advertisers to optimise their
marketing efforts and achieve a higher return on investment. [20]
2.5
Entrepreneurial concepts
2.5.1
Minimising organisational silos
Organisational silos refer to groups of individuals who are disconnected from other areas
of a company due to limited information sharing. [21] The topic is widely covered in or-
ganisational literature and considered to be ”common knowledge” why we will not go on
to explain the term.
However, minimising silo thinking in an organisation can improve a data-driven market-
ing strategy by promoting collaboration, knowledge sharing, and data integration across
different departments and teams. By breaking down silos and fostering cross-functional
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
18
==PAGE:28==
collaboration, employees can gain a better understanding of how their work contributes
to the larger organisational goals and objectives, and how their data can be leveraged to
inform and optimise marketing efforts. This can also help in identifying gaps and oppor-
tunities in the customer journey and delivering a more personalised and relevant expe-
rience. Furthermore, by integrating data from various sources, such as sales, customer
service, and marketing, teams can gain a more comprehensive and accurate view of cus-
tomer behaviour and preferences. This can lead to more targeted and effective marketing
campaigns and improve the overall customer experience. [21]
According to McKinsey, this is especially the case when it comes to CMOs. To drive
growth, it is crucial for marketing leaders to collaborate with other departments within
their organisations. This could be sales, product innovation, finance, technology, and
HR. There own research suggests that CMOs who act as ”unifiers” and work with other
executives on an equal footing are more successful at driving growth than those who
do not. These CMOs adopt the language and perspective of their C-level colleagues,
communicate clearly how marketing can meet their requirements, and make sure that
they comprehend marketing’s role. However, fostering productive relationships extends
beyond the C-levels. Marketing leaders must be able to demonstrate and establish ex-
pectations for seamless cooperation between the marketing team and other functional
units. [22]
Minimising silo thinking in an organization can greatly improve a data-driven marketing
strategy by promoting collaboration, knowledge sharing, and data integration across dif-
ferent departments and teams. Silo thinking refers to the mindset where employees tend
to focus only on their specific roles and responsibilities, leading to isolated and discon-
nected operations across various functional areas.
By breaking down silos and fostering cross-functional collaboration, employees can gain
a better understanding of how their work contributes to the larger organisational goals
and objectives, and how their data can be leveraged to inform and optimise marketing
efforts. This can also help in identifying gaps and opportunities in the customer journey
and delivering a more personalised and relevant experience.
Furthermore, by integrating data from various sources, such as sales, customer service,
and marketing, teams can gain a more comprehensive and accurate view of customer
behaviour and preferences. This can lead to more targeted and effective marketing cam-
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
19
==PAGE:29==
paigns and improve the overall customer experience.
Overall, minimising silo thinking can improve a data-driven marketing strategy by promot-
ing a culture of collaboration, knowledge sharing, and data integration, resulting in better
decision-making, increased efficiency, and ultimately, better business outcomes.
2.5.2
Hypothesis-Driven Entrepreneurship: The Lean Startup
Hypothesis-Driven Entrepreneurship is an entrepreneurial framework developed by Thomas
Eisenmann, Eric Ries and Sarah Dillard. [4] The framework builds on the theories from
the book ”The Lean Startup: How Today’s Entrepreneurs Use Continuous Innovation to
Create Radically Successful Businesses” by Eric Ries. The book was originally published
in 2011 and explores the principles of lean manufacturing and applies them to the startup
process.
The Hypothesis-Driven Entrepreneurship framework serves to validate the business model
of a new venture. When adopting the hypothesis-driven approach, the entrepreneur con-
verts their idea into testable hypotheses for their business model, which is then examined
through a sequence of MVPs. Each MVP comprises the minimum required activities to
disprove a hypothesis. Based on this data, the startup will have a better understanding
of the viability of its current business model. They can then choose to stay with their cur-
rent business model, they can pivot by changing some aspects of the model, or they can
discontinue the new venture. This process is repeated until all key business model hy-
potheses are validated. When the startup has repeated the steps for all key hypotheses,
the product-market fit is considered achieved. By adopting a hypothesis-driven approach,
entrepreneurs can mitigate the major risk of introducing a product that lacks demand in
the market. The approach maximises the utilisation of a startup’s limited resources by
limiting uncertainty before expanding its operations.
The steps of the framework are as follows:
• Step 1: Develop a vision.
• Step 2: Translate the vision into hypotheses.
• Step 3: Specify MVP Tests for the hypotheses.
• Step 4: Prioritize Tests
• Step 5: Learn from MVP Tests
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
20
==PAGE:30==
• Step 6: Persevere, Pivot, or Perish
• Step 7: Scaling and Ongoing Optimization
The framework is illustrated in figure 2.4 which originates from the referenced research
article. [4] It illustrates the six first process steps of validating the business model. The
seventh step is not illustrated in the model, however, this step is about scaling the startup
company. The author, Eric Ries, coins the term ”lean startup” to startups that are following
the principles of this approach. Unlike the common misconception, thinking that it means
a startup is bootstrapping, cutting costs as much as possible, and depending solely on
the founders’ personal resources. Instead what should be understood from the term, is to
cut spending of time and resources initially until the business model is validated. Rather,
startups should be waiting for this final step before scaling up the budget and resources,
thus, avoiding waste.
Startups should keep using hypothesis-testing methods even after validating their busi-
ness model assumptions. The focus of these tests changes from validating the business
model to improving it, consequently, creating continuous improvement. [4]
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth

==PAGE:31==
Figure 2.4: Eisenmann et al: Hypothesis-Driven Entrepreneurship: process steps

The aim of this present chapter is to provide a comprehensive overview of the methods
used in this thesis as well as a reflection on the relevance of the chosen methods in
regard to answering the tentative research questions. In section 3.3 we elaborate on
the approach for collecting the primary data source of this thesis, which consists of ten
interviews with selected CMOs/marketing leaders of Danish startup companies. In section
3.4 we present the chosen method for the data analysis and in section 3.6 we examine the
empirical context. The primary data for this thesis is collected in the timespan of October
2022 to January 2023.
3.1
Research approach and design
With the intent of answering the research questions in this thesis formulated, we are un-
dertaking an exploratory qualitative research approach. [23] Qualitative research involves
the collection, analysis, and interpretation of non-numerical data, such as words, images,
and sounds. [24] It is often used to explore and understand the individual’s experiences
and perspectives in depth, and to generate insights and meanings from these data, by
highlighting significant patterns, processes, and structural features. [23] It is particularly
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
23
==PAGE:33==
useful in fields where little is known about a topic, or where there is a need to understand
complex or nuanced phenomena. While the topic of this thesis is not unexplored or little
known, data-driven marketing is nevertheless a subject of continuous development, and
a qualitative approach is therefore expected to enhance the overall understanding. Cor-
respondingly, according to Alan Bryman, the purpose of qualitative research is to provide
a rich and nuanced understanding of the research topic and to generate new insights
and understanding that can inform theory, practice, and policy, which is what we hope to
achieve with this thesis. [24]
The research design should serve as a plan for collecting and analysing data that will help
to answer the inquired research question and to achieve the goals of the research. [23]
In this study, an exploratory research design is utilised to attain a deeper understanding
of the challenges and implications connected to data-driven marketing in Danish startup
companies. By employing an exploratory research design, the primary objective is to
attain insights into the subjects’ understanding of the topic. (Source: Malhotra N. and
Birks D., 2007.)
The aim of this thesis is to acquire new knowledge on how startups can advance their
marketing outcomes by undertaking a data-driven marketing approach. To accomplish
this ultimate goal, the research question is divided into sub-questions, where different
research methods are being utilised to answer the individual sub-questions.
1) For the purpose of uncovering the challenges startups encounter, a case study ap-
proach was employed. Ten individual interviews were conducted with informants in lead-
ing marketing roles (with part or full responsibility for the marketing activities) at Danish
startup companies. The interview questions were also designed to uncover their use of
marketing technology, best practice strategies, and relevant solutions to the identified
challenges, contributing to the recommendations presented in the following chapters.
2) With the intent of gaining a comprehensive understanding and overview of emerging
technologies and trends, relevant entrepreneurial concepts and marketing theory, we rely
on literature review as our chosen method.
We combine these two methods to form educated suggestions for possible solutions to
the identified challenges and form final recommendations in chapters 5 and 6.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
24
==PAGE:34==
3.2
Literature
The literature utilised for this thesis includes a mix of academic literature and web-based
content. While peer-reviewed research papers are to be considered of higher scientific
value and therefore also a more optimal source of information to rely on, we have found
that the academic research on this topic is somewhat limited. As mentioned in 1.2, there is
limited research that has been conducted on the topic of data-driven marketing in startups.
However, we found multiple relevant research papers on the topic of data-driven market-
ing and SMEs, which we have been able to use to gain a better understanding of the
context. We are also utilising academic literature on more general entrepreneurial topics,
nonetheless, we depend mostly on web-based content for gaining knowledge on recent
marketing technologies, digital trends and general marketing concepts. As the nonaca-
demic content is generally more exposed to biases and subjectivity, several sources and
critical assessments have been employed in the process of choosing and analysing this
content.
3.3
Interviews
To gain a comprehensive understanding of the data-driven marketing efforts Danish startup
companies undertake, semi-structured qualitative interviews were conducted with ten
startup companies in Denmark.
The interviews were conducted via online video calls, utilising the video communication
platform Google Meet [25]. All interviews were video-recorded through the platform for
the purpose of transcription and data analysis. By conducting the meetings online, we
were able to choose the most relevant companies and subjects, regardless of geographic
location, while enabling the subjects to be interviewed in familiar surroundings such as
their workplace offices or personal homes. All interviews were conducted in a timeframe
of 40-55 minutes.
Through the interviews, we wanted to cover all essential topics that relate to data-driven
marketing strategies and efforts within the companies. However, upon developing an in-
terview guide with all relevant questions, we found that each interview would potentially
take 1-2 hours to conduct, depending on the length of the informants’ responses. To
ensure that we could obtain enough informants to participate, we wanted to limit the inter-
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
25
==PAGE:35==
views to 45 minutes. Thus, we decided to make use of an existing assessment framework,
the Marketing Maturity Assessment Tool, developed by Gartner. [26] While this tool is not
designed for interview purposes, we presumed it could be efficiently integrated into the
interviews. By using this combined method, the interviewer was able to gain an overview
of where the companies are challenged and where they are outperforming, thereby inves-
tigating with follow-questions only when relevant. Below we show one of the six questions
from the Marketing Maturity Assessment Tool, to illustrate how the questions were pre-
sented.
Figure 3.1: Gartner: Example of 1/6 questions presented in the maturity assessment
3.3.1
Interview structure
Prior to conducting the interviews, an interview guide was developed, containing the fol-
lowing elements:
1) Introduction:
Obtaining consent for video recording, and a general introduction to the thesis topic as
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
26
==PAGE:36==
well as the timeline for the interview.
2) Maturity level questions:
Questions to determine the ”maturity level” of their data-driven marketing efforts to gain
an overview of possible challenges and opportunities. For each of the six questions, the
informants were given five options corresponding to the different levels of maturity. For
each question, the subjects were to pick an assessment of their current and desired ma-
turity levels. The informants were asked to elaborate on their answers whenever scoring
low or significantly high on any of the questions.
3) Questions relating to challenges:
Several in-depth questions uncover what primary challenges prevent them from becoming
more data-driven in their marketing efforts.
4) Questions relating to well-performing areas:
Questions were formulated to uncover where they outperform or believe they outperform
other companies in data-driven marketing areas.
A copy of the interview script can be found in the appendix. Using a semi-structured ap-
proach, the interview guide was not followed strictly but served merely as a structure and
direction for the interviewer. All of the informants were asked several non-scripted follow-
up questions during the interviews in order to gain a more comprehensive understanding
of the causation. By integrating and transforming survey questions that were originally
developed by Gartner, we created an overview of the current and desired maturity levels
of each company. [26]. This approach created a point of departure for further investi-
gation of the areas where the companies were scoring either low or high on the current
levels. By using this approach, we obtained large amounts of relevant information within
a short timeframe.
3.3.2
Subjects and selection criteria
We reached out to a total of 50 companies, of which ten were eventually interviewed for
this thesis. All companies were contacted through the social media platform LinkedIn,
utilising their prospect service LinkedIn Sales Navigator. [27] However, prior to this, we
identified the 50 relevant companies by applying the following filters to the Hub:1
• Danish-founded companies
1The Hub is a Danish-founded, free job search platform for jobs in startup companies. The platform allows
startups to create a company profile to advertise themselves to candidates. https://thehub.io/startups
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
27
==PAGE:37==
• 11-100 employees
• Startup stage: Growth and expansion
This produced a list of 716 filtered startups on the Hub. We then extracted companies
that were founded before 2016 as well as companies of which we had a personal bias.
Reasons for personal bias included: being a customer, history of employment at the com-
pany and close relationship with a current employee. We also extracted companies that
were either agencies or consultancies within the industries of marketing and technology.
Following that, we employed LinkedIn Sales Navigator to search for job titles within those
companies. We initially searched for ”CMO”, however, ultimately broadened our search to
include titles such as ”CCO”, ”Head of Marketing”, ”VP of Growth” and ”Chief of Growth”.
We wanted to identify employees who were responsible for the marketing efforts of the
company while also being involved, to a certain extent, in the execution of the strategy and
technology setup. This effort was cumbersome and time-consuming, however, essential
for two reasons:
• Ensuring that the informants had a broad understanding of both strategy and exe-
cution, to ensure that answers would not be affected by lack of knowledge
• Ensuring that chosen companies were at a similar growth stage where ”lack of bud-
get” would not be the primary barrier to improving their data-driven marketing ma-
turity
We eventually identified 50 relevant companies, that were all contacted through LinkedIn
Sales Navigator. Of these, 17 were interested in participating but ultimately, it was only
ossible to set up interviews with ten of these informants.
All interviewed informant companies have been founded in 2016-2020, and have between
11-100 employees, although. The interviews were conducted with the employees who
were most likely to have the most in-depth understanding of the marketing activities within
the company. The informants who were eventually interviewed hold the following titles:
Chief Commercial Officer (CCO), Chief Marketing Officer (CMO), Head of Marketing, Chief
of Growth and VP of Growth.

3.3.3
Transcription
In order to transcribe the conducted interviews, we made use of the online service Whisper
[28], as a tool to automatically transcribe audio into text, utilising artificial intelligence.
With a single exception, all interviews were conducted in English, however, the first ”pilot”
interview was conducted in Danish. The Whisper AI application translated and transcribed
the audio file, although with minor errors that were since corrected.
The video files were afterwards uploaded to the data analysis software MAXQDA, [29]
along with the derived transcription files. Various automated transcription mistakes were
corrected in the transcription files by carefully listening to each recording.
3.4
Data analysis
After correcting the automated transcription of the recorded interviews, the transcription
files were read again where potentially relevant sentences and paragraphs were high-
lighted and tentatively assigned relevant labels, using the data analysis software. This
process was highly iterative, as labels were changed as a new understanding replaced
the initial one, upon further reading of the interviews. The process bears comparison to
the contemporary understanding of the theory of the hermeneutic circle; a circular process
of interpretation in which an initial understanding informs further interpretation and that,
in turn, leads to a revision or enrichment of the initial understanding. [30]
To further analyse and interpret the collected data, we used a content analysis strategy
otherwise known as the Gioia method. [31] The Gioia method is a qualitative data analysis
method with an inductive approach. It is a grounded theory approach that ensures a
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
29
==PAGE:39==
systematic examination of evidence and facilitates the discovery of patterns in interview
data. It also serves to structure the data. It is helping researchers to: “apply systematic
conceptual and analytical discipline that leads to credible interpretations of data and also
helps to convince readers that the conclusions are plausible and defensible.” [31]
All relevant fragments and quotes from the interviews were first inserted into a tentative
data table, from where they were grouped into similar topics. Using the Gioia method, a
data structure was then created by first identifying first-order codes in the data. After sort-
ing them into first-order concepts, second-order themes were identified and then grouped
into aggregate dimensions. The data structure serves to show how theoretical constructs
are grounded in the data. To support the coding structure and show robustness in the
arguments, data tables with supporting quotes were created. After a thorough selection
process where some themes were discarded, the main themes were used to create a
grounded model that serves as a framework for the content of the following chapters.
The objective of this method was to capture the experiences of the subjects in theoretical
terms. The framework should show ”the dynamic relationships among the emergent con-
cepts that describe or explain the phenomenon of interest” [31] as well as describe the
connection between the data and the theory. [31]
The data structure and data table can be found in the appendix, the grounded model is
enclosed and explained in chapter four.
3.5
Assessment of chosen methods
This thesis seeks to identify the general challenges and enlighten the reader with insights
into relevant considerations for utilising emerging technology and ”best practise” for pur-
suing a data-driven approach to marketing. This thesis does not seek to provide specific
step-by-step instructions for how to implement a data-driven marketing approach. By util-
ising an exploratory research design instead of a conclusive one, the results of this thesis
can exclusively provide deeper insights into the topic, and the recommendations in the
following chapters should therefore also be viewed in this context. As data-driven market-
ing and technology are under continuous development and as the thesis is not focusing
on startup companies within a specific industry or customer segment, it is not relevant to
provide concrete and industry-specific recommendations.
In order to provide conclusive results and specific instructions, information needs to be
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
30
==PAGE:40==
clearly defined. A quantitative data collection process with a large representative sample
of data would have been the appropriate choice for this purpose.
3.5.1
The Gioia methodology
The chosen method for analysing the interview data is not ideal for showing variance
among the informants. The method is developed as a template for grounded theory and
with the purpose of generating process models. The data tables, which are part of the
Gioia method, are designed to highlight similarities and not differences. While the method
can be used to show variance, it requires consideration and mindfulness in the process.
Another risk of using this particular method is mixing factual evidence with interpretations
of the informants. Our considerations on this are further elaborated on in subsection 5.1.3

culture of entrepreneurship, and politics? GDPR is an important factor for data collection,
why developed countries outside of Europe might have a competitive advantage ...

through the Gioia analysis.

interview data, which constitutes our grounded model. We will first introduce the four
