4.1
Marketing maturity assessment
Based on the interview data and by utilising the pre-developed Marketing Maturity Assess-
ment Tool from Gartner, the self-assessed maturity levels of the interviewed companies
are illustrated below, along with their desired levels. The displayed scores are the average
results of six questions.
The self-assessed maturity levels of the informant companies range from level one to
level 4,5, however, only one company scores lower than 2,5. Before commencing the
interview process, a hypothesis was formed that the maturity levels of the interviewed
companies would be relatively low, with scores not exceeding two on most questions.
This hypothesis was based on the age of the companies and the sizes of their respective
teams. However, upon conducting the interviews, we were surprised to find that some
companies demonstrated higher self-assessed maturity levels, with scores of three or four
on multiple questions. There could be several explanations for this, and we will explore
these rationales further in the discussion chapter.
Figure 4.1: Self-assessed data-driven marketing maturity levels of informants
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
35
==PAGE:45==
4.2
Data-driven marketing grounded model
The data-driven marketing grounded model is developed, utilising the Gioia method, by
employing the aggregated dimensions and second-order themes derived from the inter-
view data. The model draws inspiration from other marketing models, such as the “Modern
Marketing Model” from McKinsey. [22] and the “M3 - Modern Marketing Model” developed
by Robertsen Collaborate. [32] The process model serves to illustrate the barriers and
enabling factors of data-driven marketing in startups as well as how and where these can
be addressed by the marketing leaders within the organisations.
The model consists of three primary stages on which a data-driven marketing organisation
relies. The stages are 1) Strategy; 2) Data & Analysis and 3) Execution. They are placed
in a cycle to illustrate the link between the stages as well as the continuous, iterative loop
they are in. The three stages are part of a feedback loop where data and analysis feed the
strategy with new insights, which are then implemented in the marketing plan and carried
out through execution. The execution provides new data and the cycle continues. In the
centre of the cycle, there is a fourth dimension which has an effect on the marketing efforts
throughout all three stages: Organisational Design & Culture. The three stages and the
fourth dimension constitute the four aggregated dimensions of the data structure.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
36
==PAGE:46==
Figure 4.2: The data-driven marketing grounded model
4.2.1
Aggregated dimensions
As aforementioned, the four stages/dimensions of the model consist of the aggregated
dimensions of the data structure. Each dimension is critical for the success of the data-
driven marketing organisation and affects the organisation in numerous ways.
4.2.1.1
Strategy & Planning
The marketing strategy and marketing plan describe how marketing will assist in deliv-
ering on the overall business strategy. This dimension entails positioning, targeting and
identifying ICPs and personas. Goals, KPIs and budget are also defined. Both marketing
strategy and marketing planning are highly dependent on gaining data and insights. How-
ever, marketing strategy and planning are also dependent on the organisational design
and culture. For instance, a lack of a clear company mission and goals will affect the
marketing department’s ability to create KPIs that are aligned with the overall business
goals.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
37
==PAGE:47==
4.2.1.2
Data & Analysis
First-party, second-party and third-party data can all be of relevance to a data-driven mar-
keting organisation. This dimension consists of the process of data collection, data stor-
age, data integration as well as analysis, visualisation and reporting. Customer insights
are vital for enabling a customer-centric approach and strategy, however, data collection
is not limited to the customers, but can also include competitor and market data. Data
& Analysis rely on the execution, as this is where data is collected. The organisational
design and culture also affect the marketing department’s ability to collect data. For in-
stance, the top-level management might prevent allocating data science resources to the
marketing team. Or the organisation might have procedures for developing standard op-
erating procedures (SOPs) and complex documentation for new technology utilised within
the organisation. This can affect the agility of the marketing department, making it more
rigid to act on or test new ideas.
4.2.1.3
Execution
The execution dimension is about turning data, strategy and plan into action. This in-
cludes channel activation across the entire marketing funnel. The execution dimension is
also where the customer experience strategy is activated throughout touch points in the
customer journey, often by utilising marketing automation or other marketing technology.
Customer segmentation is most often executed through a CRM system. The execution
dimension relies heavily on strategy and planning. Without a comprehensive marketing
strategy and a concrete plan for how to achieve the goals of the marketing strategy, or-
ganisations are shooting blindly into the dark. The organisational design and culture affect
the execution process in multiple ways as well. For instance, the marketing department
might not be able to execute the marketing plan and strategy, if the management man-
dates budget cuts. Or the organisational design and culture might enforce department
silos, where the marketing department has no ongoing dialogue with the sales team, the
product team or customer service.
4.2.1.4
Organisational Design & Culture
This dimension contains decisions and factors that are not defined in the marketing depart-
ment but rather by the top-level management and enforced throughout the whole organi-
sation. This includes the organisational structure and team. It also entails the role which
marketing plays inside the organisation; whether marketing is regarded as a growth-driver
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
38
==PAGE:48==
or a support function, or somewhere in between. Whether the organisation is business-
and-product-centric versus organised around customer-centricity also has a large impact
on marketing strategy and execution. [22]
4.2.2
Barriers and enabling factors
The identified second-order themes of the data structure constitute the barriers and en-
abling factors of the data-driven marketing grounded model. They are inserted into the
grounded model where they are placed relative to their connection with the four aggre-
gated dimensions. The interview findings for each theme are briefly described hereunder
and will be further examined in the discussion chapter.
4.2.2.1
Data collection
When asked about what is considered to be their biggest challenge in data-driven mar-
keting, the informant from TAKT responds that “It’s our ability to collect first-party data and
enrich the data in a way that we can drive differentiated communication in the market, and
communicate much more relevantly, so our communication becomes more effective and
converts better.” (TAKT, Extract B.1.0.70). He believes, that this is a result of consumers
becoming more concerned with user privacy as well as legislation enforcing the advertis-
ing platforms to operate accordingly. “(...) Datatilsynet1 has declared Google Analytics
illegal. So there are ways to deal with that, or work on that, but it means that the access
to data and the quality of the data is getting harder and harder.” (TAKT, Extract B.1.0.72)
The informant from TAKT also mentions GDPR as a prominent barrier to data collection.
This limits the tracking abilities across advertising platforms such as Google and Meta
where they, as a consequence of GDPR, can only track customers for seven days. He
also mentions the iOS-14 update as an important contributor, as this resulted in 90% of
all iPhone users to opt-out of tracking across other apps. Because of this, retargeting and
sophisticated segmentation of target groups has become a bigger challenge for TAKT.
(TAKT, Extract B.1.0.4)
Two other informants correspondingly mention this as a big challenge, as they rely heavily
on app tracking. As app tracking gives less user data than web tracking, it is especially
complicated to track user journeys if you sell an app-based product. The informant from
Case Company A explains that it is getting a lot harder to evaluate the sales process
1The Danish Data Protection Agency
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
39
==PAGE:49==
or channels, as it is now complicated to track purchases and attribute them to the right
channels. (Case Company A, Extract B.4.0.83)
Another experienced challenge to data collection, brought up by the informant from LifeX,
is seasonal “dirty data”. He mentions that it has been challenging for them to draw con-
clusions based on historical marketing data as the company was founded just prior to
Covid-19. The data they have available from previous years is therefore not applicable
to predicting user behaviour when adapting marketing campaigns for seasonality. (LifeX,
Extract B.3.0.30)
4.2.2.2
Data overload
Multiple informants mention “data overload” and “data abundance” as critical barriers to
adopting a more data-driven marketing approach. When asked about what critical chal-
lenges they are currently experiencing and also expect in the future, the informant from
Capturi replied:
“Probably you could kind of coin it as data abundance. There is so much data out there
that gives you sort of hints, but there’s also a lot of assumptions on all of the data points
that you have, that make it very difficult usually to actually draw any conclusion based on
it.” (Capturi, Extract B.6.0.64)
Another respondent explains that while it is uncomplicated to collect data, data overload
is forcing them to exclude some otherwise relevant data and choose which “lens to look
through” for the next 3-6 months period before deciding on key performance indicators
(KPIs). (Case Company A, Extract B.4.0.101)
The informant from Onomondo correspondingly explains that it is easy to “drown in data”.
They approach the challenge with a similar method, by looking for small “nuggets” of
information and testing based on that piece of data. Based on the data they get from that,
they then go on to try something else, to compare the data, taking an iterative approach.
She compares it figuratively to baking a cupcake and tasting it before you bake an entire
wedding cake. (Onomondo, Extract B.5.0.63)
The interviewee from Vibrant believes that part of the solution is setting the right KPIs
from the beginning and getting rid of all vanity metrics, ensuring that marketing goals are
aligned with what is “good for business”. He explains that it is necessary to understand
which data point is the key metric that you actually want to impact, and then have a good
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
40
==PAGE:50==
understanding of your KPI hierarchy. (Vibrant, Extract B.8.0.49)
4.2.2.3
Single source of truth
The informant from Capturi reasons that the main challenge to their data-driven marketing
efforts is combining information gathered from multiple sources into one source of truth.
Today, they rely on small pieces of valuable data from multiple sources, which requires a
lot of manual work to monitor across platforms. This challenge is therefore not just related
to data collection, but rather data integration. (Capturi, Extract B.6.0.50)
However, at LifeX they have developed their own Property Management System2 as part
of their product strategy. As a “technology-first” home rental company, they utilise the
software to keep track of tenant processes and — communication, room availability, con-
tracts, accounting, and much more. The software is so elaborate, that both customers
and employees use it and feed it with data daily. Because of this self-developed, com-
prehensive software, the company has managed to create that single source of truth on
which the whole organisation relies on.
“(...) it’s the one source of truth because everyone inputs all data, all information into
there. And then that is taken by the team here at LifeX and decisions are made on the
back of that, right? So if we see like occupancy is dipping or something, then you will start
investigating why is it dipping.” (LifeX, Extract B.3.0.84)
4.2.2.4
Revenue attribution
The biggest identified obstacles to revenue attribution are; 1) having limited data available,
and 2) misinterpreting available data. This was mentioned as a challenge amongst all the
informants that were interviewed for this thesis.
The informant from LegalHero also mentions “having a long sales cycle” as a problem
for working with revenue attribution. He elaborates that they have a long internal process
from when a customer initiates contact through their website until an actual sale occurs.
Not because the customers need to be nurtured or convinced to buy, but because the
quoting process within Legalhero is protracted. By the time the customer receives a price
quote and potentially decides to accept this, it can be difficult for the marketing department
to track the channels or campaigns which contributed to the sale.
Marketing automation software can be utilised to enforce a more data-driven approach.
2Property Management System, Felix: www.joinlifex.com/proptech
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
41
==PAGE:51==
However, companies with a business model that relies exclusively on low-quantity cus-
tomers cannot benefit from attribution software to the same extent. The informant from
Capturi explains this is a challenge for them, due to a lack of data points with only 60+ pay-
ing customers. Because there are numerous factors influencing the purchase decision,
he does not believe it is possible to draw significant conclusions based on an attribution
tool.
Another identified barrier to employing attribution software within Europe is the General
Data Protection Regulation (GDPR), which adds constraints to tracking and to what can
be retained at an individual user level. On top of this, there is an increasingly larger focus
on user privacy for regular consumers on a global scale – caused by consumer demands
as well as regulators. This forces major technology companies to increase user privacy.
The major internet browser companies have all made software updates to increase user
privacy, with the consequence that recognising specific user return over time is becoming
increasingly difficult. This is mentioned as a current challenge by multiple informants
during the interviews for this thesis.
These above-mentioned barriers force companies to reevaluate their attribution strate-
gies. The informant from Onomondo explains that by doing so, they now make use of
self-reported attribution rather than relying on attribution software. This tactic has re-
cently been executed on their website contact forms and product demo booking form. By
adding another mandatory input field for the potential customer to fill out, Onomondo can
get information on where the user heard about them from. If the user type ”Google”, a new
field appears. Here the user is asked: ”What did you search for?” The first field allows
Onomondo to know the channel from which the potential customer came. The second
field, which only appears when emph”Google” is typed in the first field, allows them to
know what search word or phrase was used. It also gives them an indication of whether
the last-click attribution is SEO or SEM.
”I like that approach of being more in contact with the customers, instead of trying to have
all these kinds of advanced technologies to solve your issues. I think, especially for the
attribution part. And a lot of companies are struggling with how to attribute a sale to a
channel, right? This part is so difficult to get, there are so many things that can go wrong,
as well as what kind of permissions you have. And I think it is a very neat way of solving
a fairly difficult problem in a very, very simple way.” (Onomondo, Extract B.5.0.27)
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
42
==PAGE:52==
4.2.2.5
Segmentation
Several informants point to the ”lack of resources” as a barrier that leads to limited and
insufficient segmentation. The informant from Onomondo explains that while she believes
that they have a good understanding of who the company should be targeting, the seg-
mentation and personalisation are where they are still experiencing difficulties. Although
she explains that they are using more advanced data when it comes to defining the ideal
customer profile, the company primarily works with demographic market segments.
“What we struggle with is from the kind of marketing targeting point of view, the segmen-
tation and the personalisation. How deep do we go? Do we go just industry? Do we go
job title?” (Onomondo, Extract B.5.0.18)
The informant from TAKT however, considers limited tracking abilities due to recent laws
and legislation as the biggest obstacle to segmentation. As aforementioned, the informant
is concerned with the iOS-14 update.
When mentioning the iOS 14 update, the informant is referencing an update that Apple
made in 2021. With the update, all app users from that point forward needed to opt-in, or
give permission, for any app to track their activity outside of the application. With more
than 90% of users refusing to give permission, this has affected the marketers’ ability to
obtain behavioural user data. [33] The limited ability to track behavioural data naturally
limits the ability to create behavioural segmentation campaigns, forcing marketers to rely
on demographic, geographic or, if accessible, psychographic segmentation.
4.2.2.6
ICP and persona
During the interview with Vibrant, the informant explained that identifying the company’s
buyer personas is an unfinished task and a dynamic process. He reasons that being part
of a startup, defining the company personas will always be a dynamic process. (Vibrant,
Extract B.8.0.1)
When asked about how well they know their target buyer, the informant from TAKT phrases
it the following way: “Right now, it’s one size fits all.” (TAKT, Extract B.1.0.4) With this
statement, he implies that there is currently no segmentation taking place, due to a lack of
knowledge of their ideal target group. He explains that it is primarily a question of maturity,
although they – at the time of the interview – are currently working on a more data-driven
approach to their customer profiles. He further elaborates that the challenges of data col-
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
43
==PAGE:53==
lection have caused a less data-driven approach to identifying ICP and personas. (TAKT,
Extract B.1.0.4)
For the home rental company LifeX, the informant explains that strict budget prioritisation
has limited their target customer data to demographic criteria combined with behavioural
data related to timing. Instead of utilising various types of segmentation data, they only
rely on demographics and then zoom in on the users online who are currently in the market
for housing. (LifeX, Extract B.3.0.8)
4.2.2.7
Funnel execution
As mentioned in the previous section, LifeX currently focuses entirely on customers at
the bottom of the funnel due to budget constraints. This strict budget prioritisation means
that they currently do not spend much money or resources on brand marketing. This is
unfortunate, he elaborates, as it likely deprives them of behavioural contextualisation data
that could be used to further understand their target customer and develop their ICP and
personas.
At Capturi however, the informant explains that they are exclusively investing in demand-
generation activities. In other words, top-funnel activities that aim to build brand and
problem awareness. He argues that this currently is the best prioritisation for them as low
market maturity makes it difficult to succeed with demand capture. “Because we’re build-
ing a new market that doesn’t really exist, so that’s where we put our efforts.” (Capturi,
Extract B.6.0.28)
4.2.2.8
Minimising silo mentality
We have identified two organisational factors that can act as either barriers or enabling
factors for the startups’ in their pursuit of becoming data-driven in their marketing efforts.
One of these factors is combating organisational silos or “silo mentality”, which is the lack
of information or knowledge sharing across different departments within an organisation.
The informant from LegalHero stresses the importance of continuous dialogue between
the marketing department and customer success to ensure that marketing efforts result
in the best leads. The informant from COBOD International explains the importance of
involving the sales team for the same reason.
”(...) you can be as data-driven as you want, but you won’t succeed if you don’t have the
organisational glue as part of it.” (COBOD International, Extract B.7.0.42)
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
44
==PAGE:54==
At COBOD International, he involves his sales team in the process of developing the cre-
atives for the top-funnel marketing initiatives. He explains that while the marketing team
probably have a better understanding of what is working and what is not, it is beneficial
for two reasons. First, the sales team know the customer better than anyone and has
a useful understanding of which leads turn into profitable customers. Secondly, he be-
lieves it is important to involve and engage the sales team in order to ensure their approval
and buy-in throughout the process. He also emphasises the importance of involving the
sales team in the development of dashboards to ensure that they are positive towards the
metrics on which they get measured on. (COBOD International, Extract B.7.0.42)
The B2B technology company Vibrant they are also committed to minimising the silo men-
tality in the organisation. They strive to remove the biases that the individual departments
might have by changing the outlook to a shared “Vibrant commercial perspective”. He
elaborates that this is the biggest challenge for the company right now, as it is easy to
become protective of one’s own domain. This is problematic because you need to be
agile in a startup environment where you do not necessarily know what the proven go-
to-market model is, he explains. Their approach to achieving this is for all commercial
roles to sit down in the same room, analyse the data, figure out who their ICP is, how
they acquire them, and how that acquisition can be scaled. From there on, they define
North Star commercial metrics for the entire commercial team to pursue together. (Vi-
brant, Extract B.8.0.51) Additionally, they have a shared commercial dashboard which,
he believes, enforces collaboration. Vibrant, Extract B.8.0.55)
4.2.2.9
Data-driven in agile organisations
The interview informant at Onomondo describes a persistent conflict within the organi-
sation, which is a conflict between being agile or being ”smart” and data-driven. Being
the Head of Growth, she is the gatekeeper between the growth team and the rest of the
organisation.
”(...) there’s always this balance between reacting quickly and being smart, right? And
you get this data and you want time to look at the data and think about the data. But
sometimes someone will come and say, this just happened. We need to go live now.
And a specific example is, without any notice, we’ve been asked to put together an event
with one week’s notice. And we want to say, we need time to figure out who should be
coming, how much should we be spending? And it’s like, no, we have to get this done
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
45
==PAGE:55==
now, because it impacts this, which is this and this and this. So that’s a constant battle
I think that marketing and growth people have, is that balance between being proactive
and reactive.” (Onomondo, Extract B.5.0.59).
Nonetheless, Onomondo informant also describes being ”young” and ”agile” as an ad-
vantage, as it enables them to execute in a matter of days and change things depending
on performance. She elaborates on this with an example of a pricing model that was re-
cently launched on their website. It was taken down again after just four days, as they
witnessed a large drop in website conversations. (Onomondo, Extract B.5.0.65)
The informant from LegalHero similarly explains how they use search data to influence
their decision-making when it comes to product development. While this is not accom-
plished in a strictly data-driven manner, such as employing automation strategies, they
accomplish this through cross-functional team meetings. (LegalHero, Extract B.10.0.15)
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth

In the following chapter, we will begin with undertaking a detailed examination of the self-
assessed marketing maturity levels and the limitations of the assessment. Most impor-
tantly, we will go deeper into the investigation of the barriers and enabling factors the star-
tups are encountering which were outlined in Chapter 4. Based on the interview data and
gathered literature, we will give recommendations for potential strategies and solutions
to the identified challenges. Additionally, we will investigate the ways in which existing
technology, as described in Chapter 2, may be employed to alleviate these difficulties.
Finally, we will propose an entrepreneurial framework, that was originally developed for
other purposes, for solving some of the identified barriers.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
47
==PAGE:57==
5.1
Marketing Maturity Assessment data
As mentioned in the previous chapter, we found that the self-assessed maturity levels of
the interviewed companies were higher than what was expected prior to conducting the
interviews. We were surprised to discover that some companies assessed their maturity
levels with scores of three or four in multiple dimensions. The marketing maturity assess-
ment tool is developed to assess all types and sizes of organisations, and while we do not
have the benchmark data from Gartner, it is assumed that large corporations with large
marketing teams and budgets will generally be among the highest-scoring participants.
Hereunder we try to examine the possible explanations for this.
5.1.1
Tech-first companies
Despite taking great care in selecting startup companies from diverse industries and with
different customer profiles, the prevalence of technology-based startups makes it diffi-
cult to avoid an over-representation of such companies. It is challenging to determine
the exact number of new technology-based startups, as the definition of a ”technology-
based startup” can vary and the number of startups being created is constantly changing.
Additionally, the data on the number of new startups may not be readily available or com-
prehensive. However, it is widely recognised that technology has played a significant
role in driving innovation and entrepreneurship in recent years, leading to the creation
of many new startups. Technology is a major driver of startup activity, with a significant
percentage of new startups founded in the technology sector. This includes startups in
areas such as FinTech, software development, e-commerce, cyber security, and artificial
intelligence. In 2022, the FinTech industry alone accounted for 7.1% of all startups world-
wide, while artificial intelligence and big data was the largest VC-funded startup industry
in 2022, accounting for approximately 25% of the global deals. [34]
Almost all of the companies that were interviewed can be categorised as ”tech-first compa-
nies” and several of the informants even mentioned this themselves. A tech-first company
is a business whose primary focus is on technology and relies on it to drive innovation,
growth and success. These companies prioritise technology as a core component of their
business strategy, whether that be through product development, marketing, operations
or other areas. [35] It was revealed, that several of the interviewed companies, have data
scientists employed as part of their teams and therefore can utilise these resources in the
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
48
==PAGE:58==
marketing departments. This is considered to be a significant advantage when it comes
to adopting a data-driven marketing approach.
5.1.2
Reluctancy to participate due to low maturity
Another plausible explanation for the high maturity levels might also be explained by bias.
Prior to conducting the interviews, we reached out to 50 Danish startup companies, of
which 17 were interested in participating. Ultimately, seven of these were not able to par-
ticipate for various reasons. Nevertheless, a plausible explanation for the overall high-
scoring results among the interviewees could be explained by the 22 marketing leaders
who did not wish to participate. The reluctance to participate could be caused by the fact
that they consider themselves and their organisations to be under-achieving, immature
within and unconcerned with data-driven marketing. The absence of low-scoring infor-
mants would, thus, push the overall average among participants up.
5.1.3
Subjectivity and cognitive bias
It is important to note, that the maturity levels within the companies, illustrated above in
figure 4.1, are all exclusively assessed by employees or co-founders of the respective
companies. Furthermore, the interviews and assessments have been conducted with the
employees who are fully or partly responsible for the marketing strategy and marketing
execution within their companies. The assessments are therefore by no means objective
and the informants could be influenced by cognitive bias such as the Dunning-Kruger
effect. The Dunning-Kruger effect is a cognitive bias in which people with low ability in
a particular area tend to overestimate their own competence and skills, while those with
higher ability may underestimate their own abilities. This effect occurs because individuals
with low lack the knowledge and experience needed to recognise their own incompetence,
while those with high ability may be more aware of the complexity of the task and the
potential for errors. [36]
In conclusion, it is difficult to evaluate the validity of the assessments for multiple reasons.
However, the primary purpose of the assessment was not to gain a quantitative estimate
of the maturity levels. Rather, it was employed as an interview tool for the purpose of
exposing relevant areas for further investigation within a short timeframe.
The assessment of marketing maturity levels should therefore be considered in light of
this.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
49
==PAGE:59==
5.2
Barriers and enabling factors
5.2.1
Data & Analysis
In reference to the Data and Analysis stage, we identified several barriers that are com-
plicating the data collection process for the informants and their teams. According to the
informants, this is affecting the ability to map out customer journeys, create personalised
messaging and draw on historical data for budgeting and planning purposes.
5.2.1.1
Increased focus on data drivacy
The informant from TAKT mentioned GDPR as a prominent barrier to data collection. As
the data protection regulations in Europe prevent companies to use cross-tracking for
more than seven days, this has a noticeable effect on the advertisers who are advertising
through digital advertising platforms such as Google, YouTube, LinkedIn and Facebook.
[37] For advertisers such as TAKT, this means that they have limited tracking of the users
that are engaging with their online advertising campaigns. This further affects their attri-
bution and segmentation possibilities. The attribution is affected because a website sale
can only be attributed to a given marketing campaign for seven days. If the customer re-
visits the webshop to make a purchase after eight days or longer, TAKT will not know that
the original advertising campaign contributed to the sale. This makes it more challenging
for them to calculate the ROI of their campaigns as well as map out the customer journey.
Attribution analysis can further reveal exactly which touchpoints and ads are performing
the best. Once this is established, the question is, how is it possible reach more of those
customers from the well-performing campaigns and touchpoints? The user data that can
be retrieved from these well-performing campaigns can be employed for segmenting and
enriching ICPs. However, with GDPR limiting cross-tracking opportunities, it is becoming
more difficult for companies such as TAKT to utilise campaign data for these purposes.
In regards to legislation, several informants also mentioned the iOS-14 update as a prob-
lem for them which affects their ability to employ sophisticated segmentation and retar-
geting campaigns. After Apple released the iOS-14.5 update at the end of April 2021, iOS
app developers have been required to request permission to track users beyond their own
application. [33] This resulted in a minimum of 90% of all iPhone users opt-out of cross-
tracking, which unmistakably has had a significant impact on app companies worldwide.
The informant from Case Company A explained that this affects their ability to evaluate
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
50
==PAGE:60==
the sales process and sales channels, as it is now complicated to track purchases and
attribute them to the right channels. (Case Company A, Extract B.4.0.83)
However, it is not just app development companies that are experiencing that it is becom-
ing increasingly difficult to track their users. Website analytics are also becoming more
regulated. The most commonly used website analytics tool, Google Analytics, has been
declared illegal to use in multiple European countries. The national data protection au-
thorities of these European countries have deemed that it does not comply with by GDPR.
This is also the case in Denmark, where Datatilsynet has deemed the use of Google illegal
as of July 2023. Officially, Google’s analytics tool can still legally be utilised in Denmark,
however, not without additional measures which entail utilising a reverse proxy server to
pseudonymise data, which is a complex measure. Thus, this is a serious issue that affects
a significant number of Danish companies as well as 8 out of 10 online stores, according
to Dansk Erhverv. [38]
When GDPR came into effect on May 25, 2018, it also affected the European companies
and their ability to track users through website cookies and other tracking technologies.
So was it the case, when the ePrivacy Directive came into effect. [39] This is therefore
not an unknown challenge for European startup companies. Rather, it is presumably a
continuous barrier that will only increase over time, as consumers – and thus, regulators
– become more concerned and aware of their data privacy.
The increased focus on data privacy has a directly derived effect on the data collection
process. By extension, this further affects revenue attribution, as GDPR adds constraints
to tracking and to what data can be retained at an individual user level. In fact, the in-
creasing societal focus on user privacy and by extension the emerging legislation on the
subject has an indirectly derived effect on all of the identified second-order themes, also
referred to as barriers and enabling factors.
5.2.1.2
Data nuggets and business aligned KPIs
On the other side of the spectrum, some informants report that data overload and data
abundance is even more critical barriers to adopting a fully data-driven marketing strat-
egy. Data overload refers to the state where there is an overwhelming amount of data
available in various forms. The abundance of data is both a blessing and a challenge. It
provides opportunities for marketers to extract valuable customer insights, optimise mar-
keting strategies and improve personalisation. However, when dealing with big data sets
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
51
==PAGE:61==
from multiple sources, it can be experienced as overwhelming and challenging to extract
relevant insights. Although multiple informants mention this as a significant challenge, a
few informants also indicate that they have found a possible way to minimise the chal-
lenge. Jennifer Montague, VP of Growth at Onomondo explains that she and her team
has an iterative approach to data overload. By focusing on one small nugget of interesting
data at the time, they create and test hypotheses from that. The data they get from that
then dictates the next step, whether it is doing another experiment based on that data
output or discarding it and moving on to another data nugget. In continuing in that way,
they continuously take small steps towards becoming more data-driven.
Jesper Wilhelmsen, Head of Marketing at Vibrant brings up the importance of setting the
right KPIs and eliminating vanity metrics in order to align marketing goals with business
objectives. This means that marketing activities should be designed to drive business
success, rather than simply measuring surface-level metrics that do not contribute to
meaningful outcomes. He emphasises the need to understand which data point is the
key metric that truly impacts business success. This involves identifying the KPI hierar-
chy, which refers to the levels of measurement that contribute to the ultimate goal of the
business. For example, if the business goal is to increase revenue, then KPIs related to
sales and customer acquisition would be more relevant than KPIs related to social media
likes or website traffic. This does not necessarily mean that social media followers are
to be considered a vanity metric, as this can be a contributing factor to other business
goals. This should just not be prioritised over other KPIs that are more directly linked
to revenue if this is the north star metric of the company. He believes that by focusing
on the right KPIs and understanding the KPI hierarchy, businesses can more effectively
measure the impact of their marketing efforts and make data-driven decisions that drive
overall success.
5.2.1.3
Self-reported attribution
Revenue attribution is an important dimension for data-driven marketers to master as it
can enable a better understanding of ICP and user journey as well as budget optimisation
and increased revenue. Understanding the user journey and knowing which channels and
campaigns are performing well is of the essence when having a small marketing team and
budget. However, marketers often have limited data available or get misled by the data
they rely on. This was mentioned as a challenge amongst all the informants that were
interviewed for this thesis.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
52
==PAGE:62==
Often the data that is available from common web analytics platforms, such as Google
Analytics, can be misleading and marketers will thus often attribute a sale to the last step
in the customer journey. Because of this, companies tend to have a large focus on search
engines, whether it may be paid search engine marketing (SEM) or organic search engine
optimisation (SEO). This touchpoint is often the last traceable step of the customer journey
before the customer either purchases or initiates contact with the company.
A long sales cycle is another factor that makes it difficult to attribute sales to channels,
touchpoints and campaigns. The informant from B2B company LegalHero mentioned
this as being a significant issue for them, as they have a long internal process from when
a customer initiates contact through their website until an actual sale occurs. Usually,
business-to-consumer companies do not have this problem, as they tend to have shorter
sales cycles. B2B and B2G (business to government) companies, on the other hand, can
have sales cycles that last months or even years.
The informant from Onomondo suggests that companies need to re-evaluate their at-
tribution strategies due to the aforementioned barriers. They have implemented a self-
reported attribution tactic on their website contact forms and product demo booking form.
By adding an additional mandatory, progressive input field, Onomondo can collect infor-
mation on where the user heard about them. This is providing them with an indication of
whether the last-click attribution is SEO or SEM as well as what marketing campaigns or
activities contributed to the lead or sale. The proposed action of adding extra input fields
to website forms is not an unexplored or novel idea. Nonetheless, it is an uncomplicated
method for solving a complex challenge and it inspires further reflection and exploration
of potential pragmatic ways to address data-driven challenges.
5.2.2
Execution
5.2.2.1
Full-funnel approach
Another relevant finding from the interview process shows us that startups undertake a
limited funnel execution approach due to budget restraints. Brand marketing is usually
considered a long-term investment, why it is also more challenging to measure the return
on investment. For this reason, brand marketing often gets under-prioritised in favour of
lead-generating or lead-capture efforts with a higher and more measurable ROI. One infor-
mant explained that they currently exclusively target mature customers who are showing
buying intent. Most B2B companies or B2C companies with more costly products would
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
53
==PAGE:63==
not have much success with the approach, as they would first need to nurture and gain
the trust of the customers. However, because LifeX is operating in a market with a larger
demand than supply (rental homes in large cities), this can be a sufficient prioritisation of
a very rigid budget in their current maturity level.
While this is a useful way of optimising a small budget, it also comes with other chal-
lenges. Sahil Deva, the Head of Marketing at LifeX mentions the absence of behavioural
contextualization data on their ICP as a directly derived challenge of this. Behavioural
contextualization data can be obtained when running top-funnel awareness marketing
activities, as this provides data on how potential customers interact with your content and
customer touchpoints. This behavioural data will enable you to gain a better understand-
ing of your ICP and ultimately assist in creating more personalised marketing content
moving forward.
Furthermore, the effect of engaging a customer across multiple touchpoints in the cus-
tomer journey is of significant value. According to a survey of 750 respondents, compa-
nies can increase the likelihood of the sale by three times, if they can manage to progress
the customer across their buying jobs in the customer journey. [12]
In contrast to the previously mentioned challenges of limited funnel execution, Onomondo
is an example of a company with a full-funnel and cyclical approach. In fact, at Onomondo
they go beyond the traditional sales and marketing funnel and employ the hourglass mar-
keting funnel, which is even more comprehensive than the full-funnel approach. Jennifer
Montague, Head of Growth at Onomondo explains that this is the reason why the team
she is leading holds the title of the “Growth department” rather than a traditional marketing
department. She elaborates, that marketing has a tendency to focus predominantly on
customer acquisition and generating sales leads, and therefore does not put much effort
into what happens after a sale is won. In opposition, she believes that ”Growth” offers
a more holistic approach across the whole organisation. ”We need those customers to
get new customers. We want to build loyalty and I wanted to focus more on that, kind
of combining the buyer and customer journey as opposed to just the buyer journey. As
opposed to: I hit my targets, let’s put more money in Google, right?”
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
54
==PAGE:64==
5.2.3
Organisational Design & Culture
5.2.3.1
Minimising silo mentality
Through the interviews, we identified two organisational factors that can either hinder or
enable startups in their efforts to become data-driven in marketing. One of these factors is
the presence of organisational silos. The importance of ongoing communication between
the marketing and customer success teams was emphasised by the LegalHero informant
to ensure that marketing efforts deliver the best leads. Similarly, the COBOD International
informant stresses the significance of involving the sales team for the same purpose, as
they know the customer better. They try to minimise silo mentality by incorporating sales
in marketing tasks and responsibilities. This includes shared dashboards and involving
sales in the messaging of campaigns. The informant from Vibrant also believes in having
shared dashboards across the entire organisation, to enforce a shared mission. They
aim to eliminate biases among individuals and departments by adopting a shared ”Vibrant
commercial perspective.”
This ambition of minimising department silos and silo mentality is further backed up by
literature. According to McKinsey, marketing leaders can achieve growth by collaborating
with other areas of their organisation, including sales, product innovation, finance, tech-
nology, and HR. They should seek to set expectations for seamless collaboration between
the marketing department and other functional teams. [22].
Onomondo minimises department silos in a more holistic way, as the organisational struc-
ture is less than typical. The team which the informant is responsible for constitutes
Onomondo’s ”Growth team”. The informant explained that the commercial organisation
is split into four teams: RevOps (Revenue Operations), Growth, Sales and Customer
Success. The company do not have a traditional marketing department, but rather these
cross-functional teams. Her team is a data-driven team of employees dedicated to driving
business growth. This also includes a developer, which she describes as a ”huge advan-
tage” to have as part of the team. Thus, they have a more varied set of competencies
and perspectives within the team, and they work closely with the other commercial teams
towards a shared vision.
5.2.3.2
Data-driven and organisational agility
The second identified organisational factor is organisational agility in contrast to being
data-driven. The informant from Onomondo described an ongoing conflict within the or-
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
55
==PAGE:65==
ganisation between prioritising agility or being ”smart” and data-driven. The definition
of being agile in this context is the ability of an organisation to adapt and succeed in a
rapidly changing environment while maintaining a stable foundation. Especially estab-
lished companies often struggle to become more dynamic, whereas startup companies
are often considered to have a competitive advantage as they often are usually more agile
[40]
However, if the startup company is to ”renew itself, adapt, change quickly, and succeed
in a rapidly changing, ambiguous, turbulent environment” [40] how do they also enable
a data-driven culture? These seem to be contradicting concepts. To be data-driven in
marketing requires allocating time and resources for testing, tracking and reporting. To
continually evaluate marketing efforts, employing data findings to improve.
The informant from LegalHero provides an example of how they manage to do both. He
explains how they in LegalHero utilise search data to influence their decision-making when
it comes to product development. In other words, they work cross-functionally by gath-
ering marketing data in the marketing department and utilising it in the product team to
improve their product. This is an excellent example of how minimising silo mentality can
lead to being both agile and data-driven at the same time.
However, from a data-driven marketing standpoint, there are also benefits of having an
agile organisational culture. Agility within the organisation affects how quickly data find-
ings can be implemented. The informant from Onomondo argues for both sides, as she
explains how fast execution and decision-making can have positive consequences for
testing hypotheses and implementing data findings.
5.2.3.3
Conclusion of discussion
We propose that startups take a full-funnel approach to their data driven marketing. By
utilising a data-driven marketing engine which entails the right data technology stack and
automation processes, a full-funnel approach can be achieved with fewer resources as
well as adhering to trim budgets.
However, developing a customised marketing engine and marketing technology stack is a
timely task, that requires vast amounts of research, and inputs from internal and external
stakeholders as well as a dedicated employer who can project manage and own the pro-
cess. In smaller startups with few marketing resources, this process might even require
limiting or completely de-prioritising ongoing campaigns or lead-generating initiatives for
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
56
==PAGE:66==
a period of time, solely focusing on building the marketing engine, with the prospect of
generating more and better sales-qualified leads in the future.
Thus, we suggest that they undertake the Hypothesis-Driven Entrepreneurship approach
which according to Eisenmann et al. allows startups to get the most information possible
for each resource used, to resolve uncertainty. While this theory is developed for validating
business models, we believe it can be successfully employed to validate the data-driven
marketing customer journey.
We propose that startups create an initial ICP and map out their customer journey, where
they identify relevant touchpoints. Following, we suggest employing the hypothesis-driven
entrepreneurship framework, by using the steps to validate each touchpoint and accompa-
nying campaign or activity. This can furthermore be done with the ICP and the marketing
technology stack.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
57
==PAGE:67==
6
Conclusion
With the continual emergence of new technology and the increasing use of data as a
currency, there is a growing societal focus on the need for data privacy. Companies are
not allowed to track users to the same extent as they used to and although they employ
complex technology, they become more constrained by the legislation. We interviewed
ten marketing leaders and sought to analyse how startup companies can become more
data-driven in their marketing approach in a data-regulated world.
The strengthened data regulations limit startup companies’ ability to collect data. This in-
directly affects their data-driven marketing efforts such as funnel execution, segmentation,
developing ICPs and buyer personas, revenue attribution and creating a single source of
truth.
Another identified challenge is limited funnel execution. We identified that some of the
informants prioritise only top-of-the-funnel activities, while others strictly prioritise button-
funnel campaigns and activities. Limited top-funnel execution and a lack of focus on brand
marketing deprive the company of behavioural contextualization data which could have
otherwise been utilised to further develop their ICP. Full funnel execution is essential, as
studies show that it can increase the likelihood of a sale by three times.
With this thesis, we argue that a full-funnel approach could solve many of the identi-
fied challenges. By utilising a data-driven marketing engine which entails the right data
technology stack and automation processes, a full-funnel approach can be achieved with
fewer resources and adhering to trim budgets.
However, as this is an unrealistic scenario for many less mature startup companies, we
suggest an alternative solution. The startups might undertake the Hypothesis-Driven En-
trepreneurship approach which, according to Eisenmann et al. [4], helps startups resolve
uncertainty by getting the most information possible for each used resource.
We also propose that startups take smaller and more pragmatic initiatives to collect mar-
keting data, such as self-reported attribution, which was utilised by informant company
Onomondo.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
58
==PAGE:68==
Such pragmatic initiatives, while maybe not as small, can also include organisational ini-
tiatives. The informant from LegalHero showed us how an organisation can balance agility
with being data-driven by minimising silo mentality. When achieving this, they can utilise
data across departments to improve products and revenue.
We suggest that startups further seek to minimise silo mentality by creating shared dash-
boards across the entire organisation and creating KPIs for the data-driven marketing
strategy that are directly linked to the general business goals. Overall, by fostering a cul-
ture of collaboration, knowledge sharing, and data integration, startups can enhance a
data-driven marketing strategy, leading to better decision-making, increased efficiency,
and ultimately, better business results.
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth

to an island that doesn’t look sexy or the sun is not shining or it’s not worth going there.
So instead of spending a lot of money on getting new customers in, I would rather make
sure everything looked nice. So the website, our profile, everything. So we spent the first
months on that and n the ow we are spending time on the whole sales funnel. So a part
of that has been to professionalize the SEO optimization and now the SEM optimization
as well. So getting the flows in place and also getting the right tools to get it done. One
thing that is often forgotten is the integration between system and then the organization.
I think that’s very often forgotten how you make a change in a company. Like I have a
sales organization naturally and they need to be a part of it, whereas if you have less
mature marketing people, they maybe just run towards the direction by HubSpot because
that’s the best system and then you just run. But if you lose your whole sales organization,
they’re not a part of the process, so that has been very important. So that’s kind of some
soft pieces. So you can be as data-driven as you want, but you won’t succeed if you don’t
have the organizational glue as part of it. And I think that’s something that’s maybe worth
for you also to have in mind is that if you have a perfect upper funnel marketing optimiza-
tion, that’s very nice, but if the sales guys are not following up on the leads, it doesn’t
matter anyway. So that’s been very important for me, has been to have the sales guys
in the process of choosing our system and also being a part of developing the creatives
that we do as the first educational layer and all that, even though we probably have a
marketing better understanding of what’s working and what’s not, that they feel a part and
engage in the process. And once we have the dashboards also evaluating the sales guys
at the sales meetings, that they are positive towards these numbers. Otherwise, they will
shoot down the whole setup. So I think that’s really important and often forgotten as an
essential part of practicing data-driven marketing.

No, we actually use data to remove all the different biases that might be in terms of: I
have a marketing mindset, somebody in sales has sales, somebody partners. But we
look at it from a Vibrant commercial perspective. So, I would say the biggest challenge to
overcome is actually aligning all commercial roles, teams, KPIs into looking at it from the
same point of view. So, everything that we do is looked at as a commercial growth driver,
not to make marketing a success or sales a success, but to make Vibrant into a success.
And that sounds like a no-brainer, but it actually can be a bit tricky. Because you often
Data-driven Marketing for Startup Growth
183
==PAGE:193==
have it like where you can become a bit protective of your own little success story, but you
need to be super agile, at least in a startup environment, because you don’t necessarily
know what your proven go-to-market model is. So, you need to get into a room together,
all commercial roles, look at the data that you have been given, who is actually our ideal
customer profile, how do we acquire them, how can we scale that acquisition and then
work together. And then you come up with the different KPIs and data points that you
want to track. Because otherwise, you can have a department that changes one data
KPI metric and another one, and then at some point, we all have just scattered all over
the world. So, you need to define these North Star commercial metrics that is the ones
that you chase together. And then the rest just becomes translations into activities from
the different departments. So, just uniting all commercial aspects of the company into the
same data structure.
